CHAPMAN and Others v LUMINIS PTY LTD and Others (No 4)*
[2001] FCA 1106

von Doussa J

South Australia District Registry

Trade Practices — Misleading or deceptive conduct — Services — Supply to
Commonwealth — Professional services — Conduct to be in trade or
commerce — Reports pursuant to agreements made in trade or commerce
— One report to Minister pursuant to statutory function — Other report
by expert to Ministerial reporter — Statements made in reports not
conduct in trade or commerce — Trade Practices Act 1974 (Cth), ss 6(2),
52(1) — Fair Trading Act 1987 (SA), s 56 — Fair Trading Act 1985
(Vic), s 11.

Trade Practices — Damages — Cause of action assigned by corporation
alleged to have suffered loss — Cause of action not assignable and action
not maintainable by assignees — Trade Practices Act 1974 (Cth), s 82(1)
— Fair Trading Act 1987 (SA), s 84 — Fair Trading Act 1985 (Vic), s 37.

Trade Practices — Limitation of actions — Damages — Applicant joined in
1998 three years after accrual of cause of action — Action barred — Time
not extended — Amendment to join not allowed — Trade Practices Act
1974 (Cth), 82(2) — Judiciary Act 1903 (Cth), s 79 — Limitation of
Actions Act 1936 (SA), ss 38A, 48(1), 55(1), 56 — Limitation of Actions
Act 1958 (Vic), s 34 — Federal Court Rules 1979 (Cth), O 6, r 2, O 13,
r 2.

Negligence — Duty of care — Person reporting to Minister — Reporting to
Minister pursuant to statute — Minister making political decision provided
for by statute — Duty of care to persons liable to suffer economic loss
absent — Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Heritage Protection Act
1984 (Cth), s 10.

Aborigines — Heritage protection — Notice under Aboriginal and Torres Strait
Islander Heritage Protection Act 1984 (Cth), s 10(3) bad — Reporter and
Minister exercising functions and powers — Civil liability absent —
Application for s 10(1) declaration — Form of — Representations —
Minister’s duty to consider — Extent and nature of — Minister mistaken
as to duty — Minister’s knowledge of duty judged according to what he

*[EDITOR’S NOTE: See also 86 FCR 513, 100 FCR 229 and 104 FCR 368.]

63
123 FCR 62]
CHAPMAN v LUMINIS (No 4)

thought position was at the time, not later curial decisions — Aboriginal
and Torres Strait Islander Heritage Protection Act 1984 (Cth), s 10.

Aborigines — Aboriginal tradition — Nature, notoriety, dynamic and protean
quality of — Incomprehensibility to non-Aborigines — Aboriginal and
Torres Strait Islander Heritage Protection Act 1984 (Cth), s 3.

Torts — Statutory duty — Breach of — Procedural failure — No private right
of action for damages — Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Heritage
Protection Act 1984 (Cth), s 10.

Torts — Misfeasance in public office — Elements of.

Acquisition of Property — Acquisition of land — Otherwise than on just terms
— By Commonwealth — Declaration under Aboriginal and Torres Strait
Islander Heritage Protection Act 1984 (Cth), s 10(1) — Declaration set
aside on judicial review — Declaration not acquisition of property nor
law with respect to acquisition of property — Constitution of the
Commonwealth, s 51(xxxi) — Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander
Heritage Protection Act 1984 (Cth), s 28.

Partly, s 10 of the Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Heritage Protection Act
1984 (Cth) (the Act) provided:
‘‘(1) Where the Minister:
(a)
receives an application made orally or in writing by or on behalf
of
an
Aboriginal
or
a
group
of
Aboriginals
seeking
the
preservation or protection of a specified area from injury or
desecration;
(b)
is satisfied:
(i)
that the area is a significant Aboriginal area; and
(ii)
that it is under threat of injury or desecration;
(c)
has received a report under subsection (4) in relation to the area
from a person nominated by him and has considered the report and
any representation attached to the report; and
(d)
has considered such other matters as he thinks relevant;
he may make a declaration in relation to the area.’’
Before a person could submit a report for the purposes of s 10(1)(c) the Minister
had to publish a notice containing the matters set out in s 10(3)(a). By s 10(3)(b)
he had to give due consideration to representations received. For s 10(1)(c), a
s 10(4) report had to deal with matters set out in s 10(4), including the particular
significance of the area to Aboriginals. For an area to be a significant Aboriginal
area it had to be one of particular significance to Aboriginals in accordance with
Aboriginal tradition. By s 3 ‘‘Aboriginal tradition’’ was defined as:
‘‘The body of traditions, observances, customs and beliefs of Aboriginals
generally or of a particular community or group of Aboriginals, and includes
any such traditions, observances, customs or beliefs relating to particular
persons, areas, objects or relationships.’’
A s 10 declaration had to be laid before both Houses of the Commonwealth
Parliament. It was liable to be disallowed. Its status was the same as a regulation.
By s 9 the Minister could make a declaration for a period not exceeding 30 days.
The Act provided for a protective mechanism of last resort where State or
Territory legislation was ineffective or inadequate to protect heritage areas or
objects. The remedial effect of the Act, and the importance of preserving and
protecting Aboriginal culture, were matters of national interest transcending private
proprietary and economic interests of individuals affected by protection.
The Act did not provide the Minister with immunity from civil action.

64
FEDERAL COURT REPORTS
[(2001)

B Co wanted to develop land on an island off the coast of South Australia (the
State) as a recreational and tourist resort including a marina complex (the marina).
Vehicular access was by ferry. The access was inadequate. Access to the island
would have been improved by a bridge from the mainland to the island.
The development was to be in two stages. Planning approval was granted by the
Local Council (the Council) conditional on Stages 2-6 not proceeding until
practical completion of the bridge.
By 1990 B Co was unable to pay its principal financier whose security included
a mortgage over the marina. Finance for Stage 1 was declined and payment of
moneys owing was sought. B Co was given time to pay.
The State agreed with the financier to procure construction of the bridge if the
financier financed Stage 1.
In 1991 B Co was insolvent and a scheme of arrangement was entered into.
In late 1991 Stage 1 was opened.
In early 1993 the State, the Council and B Co made an agreement (the Tripartite
Agreement) under which the State Minister was to procure construction of the
bridge.
In the beginning, generally, there was not much opposition to the construction of
the bridge. In early 1993 opposition grew.
In May the financier refused to finance Stage 2.
Work on the bridge started in October 1993. In that month the Lower Murray
Aboriginal Heritage Committee (LMAHC) wrote to the relevant Minister of the
Commonwealth (the Minister) expressing grave concern. Later correspondence
included a letter of 23 December 1993 to the Minister in which LMAHC requested
the Minister to consider using his s 10 power to protect sites affected by the
construction of the bridge, should the State Minister not exercise powers to protect
them under s 24 of the Act.
In March 1994 the financier appointed receivers and managers of B Co.
In April 1994 the State Minister told the Minister that construction of the bridge
would proceed.
Two letters in April 1994 to the Minister largely overtook the letter of
23 December 1993.
In early May 1994 the financier’s opposed application to wind up B Co was
adjourned, and a s 9 declaration was made, which was later extended.
Later in May 1994 the Minister nominated a respondent (S) to report under
s 10(4). The nominating document described the letter of 23 December 1993 as the
s 10 application.
A notice was published in purported compliance with s 10(3)(a).
The Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Commission (ATSIC) employed S to
prepare a s 10(4) report. S was to perform the services to be provided at a high
standard. ATSIC was to pay her daily fees and expenses.
From June 1994 the Aboriginal Legal Rights Movement (ALRM) instructed a
respondent (L Co), a consultancy service provider, and another respondent (F) in
relation to the matter. By late June L Co’s and F’s instructions came to be to write
a report containing an anthropological evaluation to be forwarded as part of an
ALRM submission to S. F was to use her professional skills exclusively in the
interests of the ALRM to interpret the material provided to her by them and to
formulate the case for threatened injury and desecration to advance their interest,
which was to stop the construction of the bridge.
From one Aboriginal woman (K) F received information said to be esoteric
knowledge confined to women. K received the knowledge in about 1954 from a
woman born in 1894. It was implicit in K’s evidence that the knowledge she
received was part of an oral tradition handed down by female kin of preceding
generations. F wrote down the information she received, wrote an evaluation of it,
and appended the documents to her report in sealed envelopes (the secret
envelopes).

65
123 FCR 62]
CHAPMAN v LUMINIS (No 4)

The report recommended the need for protection.
In her report S concluded the bridge would affect a significant Aboriginal area
for reasons including that the bridge site was within a general area regarded by
women of the relevant Aboriginal people as crucial to the reproduction of those
people. Such was the subject of the secret envelopes. S reported she was satisfied
the applicants understood the general nature of the issues.
On 7 July 1994 S’s report together with representations received by her, which
exceeded 400, were delivered into the Minister’s office. He was in contact with his
ministerial assistant and received formal advice from ATSIC. B Co and the
applicants, by their solicitors, received a copy of S’s report.
On 8 July 1994 the Minister read S’s report and other material but probably not
F’s report. B Co received a copy of F’s report without the contents of the secret
envelopes and faxed a submission to the Minister.
The Minister did not read each of the representations received by S. The
Minister knew of Tickner v Bropho (1993) 40 FCR 183 at the time it was
published in 1993 where, at 194, it is said a Minister has to consider
representations attached to a report as a precondition to making a s 10 declaration,
but he did not understand he was personally to read every representation or to give
the representations the degree of personal attention which Tickner v Chapman
(1995) 57 FCR 451 was later to decide he should have. He understood he was
entitled to have his advisers read the representations and bring to his attention the
substance of them.
On 9 July 1994 the Minister declared under s 10.
On 10 July 1994 the declaration was published. It prohibited construction of the
bridge for 25 years.
In August 1994 an order winding up B Co was made and a liquidator appointed.
In December 1995, the appeal against the quashing of the decision to declare
under s 10 and S’s report was dismissed (Tickner v Chapman). The Full Court
upheld the single judge’s decisions that the notice was bad and that the Minister
had not considered the representations before declaring.
Later in December 1995 a female Royal Commissioner reported that the
knowledge said to be esoteric was fabricated to obtain a s 10 declaration and to
prevent construction of the bridge.
In June 1996, in relation to a fresh s 10 application, a female judge, whose
nomination to report was held to be invalid, reported the applicants failed to
provide adequate material to support the making of a declaration.
On 22 May 1997 the Commonwealth Parliament legislated to allow construction
of the bridge.
On 22 May 1997 B Co’s liquidator assigned to the applicants causes of action it
claimed to have against L Co, F and S (the first assignment) under s 477(2)(c) of
the Corporations Law.
On 23 May 1997 this proceeding commenced against L Co, F and S.
In September 1997 the financier, by the receivers and managers of B Co, entered
into possession of the marina. On 25 September 1997 the receivers and managers
of B Co sold the marina to K Co the trustee of trusts of which the applicants were
beneficiaries.
On 25 September 1997, B Co’s liquidator assigned to the applicants causes of
action it claimed to have against the Minister and the Commonwealth under
s 477(2)(c) of the Corporations Law.
Later the Minister and the Commonwealth were joined as respondents.
In September 1998 on motion treated as one made under O 6, r 2 of the Federal
Court Rules 1979 (Cth) (the Rules) and filed in May 1998, B Co was joined as an
applicant.
Construction of the bridge started in 1999.
Partly and in substance, it was contended that but for the s 10 declaration (on the
making of which immediate loss was contended to have been suffered)

66
FEDERAL COURT REPORTS
[(2001)

construction of the bridge would have started again, the financier would have
exercised its power of sale in late 1994 and applied the proceeds to reduce B Co’s
debt. Because the bridge had not been constructed, the price of the sale to K Co
was low. The difference between the price that would earlier have been obtained
and the price obtained on the sale to K Co, and the interest B Co continued to
incur to the financier until the sale to K Co, were claimed by the applicants under
the assignments from B Co.

Of claims alleging contravention of s 52(1) of the Trade Practices Act 1974
(Cth) (the TPA), s 56 of the Fair Trading Act 1987 (SA) and s 11 of the Fair
Trading Act 1985 (Vic)

Partly, s 6(2) of the TPA provided:
‘‘This Act . . . has . . . the effect it would have if:
(a)
any references . . . to trade and commerce were . . . confined to
trade or commerce:
. . .
(iv) by
way
of
the
supply
of
goods
or
services
to
the
Commonwealth or an authority or instrumentality of the
Commonwealth.’’
‘‘Services’’ was relevantly defined to include:
‘‘any rights . . . benefits, privileges or facilities that are, or are to be,
provided . . . in trade or commerce, and without limiting the generality of the
foregoing, includes the rights, benefits, privileges or facilities that are, or are
to be provided . . . under:
(a)
a contract for or in relation to:
(i)
the performance of work (including work of a professional
nature) . . .’’
Order 13, r 2 of the Rules was amended in 1994 to modify the rule in Weldon v
Neal (1887) 19 QBD 394 to allow amendments in the circumstances in subrr
(4)-(7).
Held: (1) The extended operation of s 52 given by s 6(2) does not remove the
requirement that conduct proscribed by s 52 must be in trade or commerce. (174)
R v Australian Industrial Court; Ex parte CLM Holdings Pty Ltd (1977)
136 CLR 235 at 244; Concrete Constructions (NSW) Pty Ltd v Nelson (1990)
169 CLR 594 at 602-604, applied.
Obiter. In the definition of services the words following ‘‘without limiting the
generality of the foregoing’’ give an expanded meaning to the words ‘‘rights . . .
benefits, privileges of facilities’’ but all the services remain subject to the ‘‘in trade
or commerce’’ qualification. (175)
(2) The making of the statements in S’s report was not conduct by S in trade or
commerce. (178)
Concrete Constructions (NSW) Pty Ltd v Nelson (1990) 169 CLR 594 at 604-
605; Unilan Holdings Pty Ltd v Kerin (1992) 35 FCR 272; Giraffe World Australia
Pty Ltd v Australian Competition and Consumer Commission [1999] ATPR 41-
669; Robin Pty Ltd v Canberra International Airport Pty Ltd [1999] ATPR 41-710,
applied.
(3) S’s conduct in performing her function as a reporter, incidental to the making
of her report, did not bear a trading or commercial character and could not have
been conduct in trade or commerce. (180)
Bond Corporation Pty Ltd v Thiess Contractors Pty Ltd (1987) 14 FCR 215,
discussed.
Durant
v
Greiner
(1990)
21
NSWLR
119;
Prestia
v
Aknar
(1996)
40 NSWLR 165, distinguished.
(4) That L Co carried on business as a provider of consultancy services and
F used her professional skills in the course of the consultancy did not establish that

67
123 FCR 62]
CHAPMAN v LUMINIS (No 4)

F’s conduct was conduct in trade or commerce for the purposes of the statutory
prohibitions. (186)
(5) In negotiating with and entering into the contractual arrangements between
L Co and the ALRM for the provision of F’s services, L Co and F engaged in
conduct in trade and commerce. But that conduct bore a character different from
the product of the consultancy service. (188)
Prestia v Aknar (1996) 40 NSWLR 165 at 193, followed.
(6) The making of the impugned representations by F in her report was not
conduct in trade or commerce. (190)
Plimer v Roberts (1997) 80 FCR 303 at 327, applied.
(7) It not having been established on the balance of probabilities that the esoteric
knowledge as reported in F’s and S’s reports was fabricated or was not in truth part
of the Aboriginal tradition within the meaning of the Act, the claims against L Co,
F and S failed. (199)
(8) The causes of action alleged under s 82 of the TPA and the corresponding
provisions of the Fair Trading Acts were not maintainable by the applicants suing
as assignees of B Co because they were not persons within the terms of the
statutory remedies. (205, 207)
Park v Allied Mortgage Corporation Ltd [1993] ATPR 46-105 at 53,469;
Allstate Life Insurance Co v Australia and New Zealand Banking Group Ltd
(unreported, Federal Court, Beaumont J, No G 381 of 1994, 7 November 1994);
National Mutual Property Services (Australia) Pty Ltd v Citibank Savings Ltd
(1995) 132 ALR 514 at 539; Brookfield v Davey Products Pty Ltd (unreported,
Federal Court, Branson J, No SG 112 of 1993, 8 February 1996) at p 28, followed.
Pritchard v Racecage Pty Ltd (1997) 72 FCR 203, applied.
(9) On the footing that leave to join B Co was granted under O 6, r 2 of the
Rules, B Co’s causes of action were statute barred. (209)
(10) None of subrr (4)-(7) of O 13, r 2 of the Rules applied to the joinder of
B Co. (212-215)
(11) The applicants contending there was an immediate loss on the making of
the s 10 declaration, time ran from then, even though the extent of that loss might
thereafter have increased or decreased on the happening of later events. (219)
Wardley Australia Ltd v Western Australia (1992) 175 CLR 514 at 527,
distinguished.
(12) Section 48 of the Limitations of Actions Act 1936 (SA) (allowing for
extension of time) was not picked up and rendered a surrogate federal law to
operate beside s 82 of the TPA by s 79 of the Judiciary Act 1903 (Cth). (220)
John Robertson & Co Ltd (In liq) v Ferguson Transformers Pty Ltd (1973)
129 CLR 65 at 80, 83-84, 88, 94, applied.
Vink v Schering (No 2) [1991] ATPR 41-073, distinguished.
(13) Section 34 of the Limitation of Actions Act 1958 (Vic) (abrogation of the
rule in Weldon v Neal) is not addressed to joinder of a new party where the cause
of action against that party is out of time. It had no application here where the
party entitled to maintain the statutory cause of action, B Co, was already out of
time when the application to join it was made. (226)

Of claims alleging negligence
(14) The Minister and S would have had a duty of care to B Co had imposition
of such a duty been consistent with their powers and functions under the Act. (243)
Perre v Apand Pty Ltd (1999) 198 CLR 180 at 217, 302, 325; Crimmins v
Stevedoring Industry Finance Committee (1999) 200 CLR 1 at 13, 16, 18-19, 23-
25, 26, 29-30, 37-42, 59-62, 72, 113; Graham Barclay Oysters Pty Ltd v Ryan
(2000) 102 FCR 307 at 391, 454, applied.
(15) The decision required of the Minister under s 9 or 10 was of a political
nature. (260)
Minister for Aboriginal Affairs v Peko-Wallsend Ltd (1986) 162 CLR 24 at

68
FEDERAL COURT REPORTS
[(2001)

63-64; Wilson v Minister for Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Affairs (1997)
189 CLR 1 at 19, applied.
(16) The Minister did not owe private law duties of care to B Co in connection
with his exercises of the powers under ss 9 and 10. (263, 265)
Crimmins v Stevedoring Industry Finance Committee (1999) 200 CLR 1 at 13,
20, 21, 62, 86, 101-102, 113, applied.
(17) S did not owe a private law duty of care to B Co. (268)
Wilson v Minister for Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Affairs (1997)
189 CLR 1 at 18, 19, applied.

Of other issues under the Act
Of the notice being bad,
(18) A construction of s 10(3) by which the notice would have been good was
not so patently untenable that the inadequacy of the notice should have been
known to the Minister and S. (271, 677)
Dunlop v Woollahra Municipal Council [1982] AC 158, applied.
Of the requirements of a s 10 application,
(19) A s 10 application need not be by one discrete communication. A s 10
application may be constituted by communications which progressively add to the
description of the area for which protection is requested, change the boundaries of
the area, and enlarge on the information given as reason for fearing injury or
desecration. (272)
(20) By the time the Minister exercised his powers under ss 9 and 10 the
application included the letters of 23 December 1993 and the two in April 1994.
(272)
(21) The important thing is that once the Minister decides to act, the substance
of the application, however made to that time, becomes fixed in the sense that it is
from the information then comprising the application that the area specified by it
and the purpose of it must be ascertained and published in the notice. (272)
Of the Minister’s obligation to consider representations,
(22) Section 10(1) did not require the Minister to read each and every
representation received by S before he decided to declare under s 10.
Tickner v Chapman (1995) 57 FCR 451 at 461-462, 476-478, 493, 494, 497;
Minister for Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander Affairs v Western Australia
(1996) 67 FCR 40, explained.
(23) The Minister’s knowledge about what was necessary under s 10(1) to
consider the representations was to be judged according to what he thought was his
position at the time, not according to later curial decisions. The Minister was
mistaken as to the requirement to consider the representations but he was not
recklessly indifferent or wilfully blind to whether he was acting within power
when he declared under s 10 without first reading or giving closer attention to the
representations. (274, 677)
Of Aboriginal tradition,
(24) The information given to F and S provided a reasonable basis for their
opinions that a number of people held the relevant belief. The knowledge of which
K spoke was part of the body of traditions, observances, customs and beliefs of the
relevant people within the definition of Aboriginal tradition in s 3. (275)
(25) In the definition of Aboriginal tradition ‘‘traditions, observances, customs
or beliefs’’ together carry the notion that there has been a handing down from
generation to generation in accordance with the accepted understanding of the way
in which Aboriginal history, laws, observances and practices have been transmitted
through the aeons. (275)
Obiter. (a) There may be Aboriginal tradition where one person only knows of
the particular tradition, observances, custom or belief. (275)
(b) An area could qualify as a significant Aboriginal area even though all the
descendant members of the community who were the native title holders of the

69
123 FCR 62]
CHAPMAN v LUMINIS (No 4)

area have died if before that occurred the Aboriginal tradition that rendered the
area significant had been properly recorded, and the tradition is still acknowledged
and respected by other members of the Aboriginal community. (275)
(26) (a) If the circumstances specified in s 3(2)(a) exist, an area is to be deemed
to be desecrated without need for demonstration of reasoning satisfying a non-
Aboriginal mind as to why one of those circumstances would cause harm. (393)
(b) The reason why a proposed activity gives rise to a threat of injury or
desecration must be addressed but, depending on the nature and content of the
Aboriginal tradition involved, it may not be possible for a reporter or Minister to
go beyond receiving a description of the beliefs constituting the tradition and
forming a view about the depth, sincerity and importance of those beliefs.
(394, 396)
(27) Aboriginal tradition within the Act is dynamic. It may innovate to
accommodate change. (397)
Members of Yorta Yorta Aboriginal Community v Victoria (2001) 110 FCR 244
at 254-260 [27]-[49], applied.
(28) Between F and B Co there was a sufficiently close relationship to give rise
to a duty of care if such a duty and its content were coincident with the work to be
done by her and the scope of the duty owed to ALRM. (285)
Hill v Van Erp (1997) 188 CLR 159 at 167, applied.
(29) F did not owe a duty of care to B Co either to carry out the preparation of
her report with reasonable care to prevent B Co suffering loss by the making of a
s 10 declaration or to ensure that sufficient tests and all proper investigations were
done to test the truthfulness of the esoteric knowledge related to her. (300)
Ross v Caunters [1980] Ch 297 at 322; Hill v Van Erp (1997) 188 CLR 259 at
171, 186-187, 193, 196, 211, 236; Sutherland Shire Council v Heyman (1985)
157 CLR 424 at 503; Bryan v Maloney (1995) 182 CLR 609 at 618; Perre v Apand
Pty Ltd (1999) 198 CLR 180 at 200, 220, 224, 258, 290, 299, 328, applied.

Of breach of statutory duty

(30) On its proper construction, the Act does not create a private right of action
for damages in one claiming to be aggrieved by procedural failure. (687)
Sovar v Henry Lane Pty Ltd (1967) 116 CLR 397 at 405; Byrne v Australian
Airlines Ltd (1995) 185 CLR 410 at 424; Pyrenees Shire Council v Day (1998)
192 CLR 330 at 347; X (Minors) v Bedfordshire County Council [1995] 2 AC 633
at 731-732, applied.

Of misfeasance in public office

(31) Bad faith not having been shown against them, and they believing they
acted lawfully at all times, neither the Minister nor S was guilty of the tort.
(696, 708)
Northern Territory v Mengel (1995) 185 CLR 307; Sanders v Snell (1998)
196 CLR 329 at 344-346; Three Rivers District Council v Governor and Company
of Bank of England [2000] 2 WLR 1220 at 1231, 1269; [2000] 3 All ER 1 at 8, 43-
44, applied.

Of the claim for compensation against the Commonwealth under s 28 of the
Act (declaration under Pt II resulting in acquisition of property otherwise
than on just terms)

(32) There was no acquisition because the decision to declare under s 10 was to
be treated as invalid from when it was made. (714-716)
Wattmaster Alco Pty Ltd v Button (1986) 13 FCR 253 at 257-258; 70 ALR 330
at 335, applied.
(33) Section 28 did not apply because the s 10 declaration was bad and, so, not
made under Pt II. (717)

70
FEDERAL COURT REPORTS
[(2001)

First English Evangelical Lutheran Church v County of Los Angeles (1987)
482 US 304 at 391, distinguished.
Obiter. (a) Assuming, contrary to the holding, the s 10 declaration operated,
prohibition of the construction of the bridge and associated earth works did not
result in the Commonwealth acquiring the identifiable proprietary interest under or
as a result of the declaration or an identifiable benefit or advantage relating to the
ownership or use of property. (773, 734)
Commonwealth v Tasmania (Tasmanian Dam Case) (1983) 158 CLR 1 at 145-
146, 181-182, 247-248, 283; Mutual Pools & Staff Pty Ltd v Commonwealth
(1994) 179 CLR 155 at 172-173, 185; Georgiadis v Australian and Overseas
Telecommunications Corporation (1994) 179 CLR 297 at 304-305; Smith v ANL
Ltd (2000) 204 CLR 493 at 499-500, 505-506, 511-512, 527-528; 176 ALR 449 at
451, 456, 461, 474; Waterhouse v Minister for Arts and Territories (1993) 43 FCR
175 at 192; Bienke v Minister for Primary Industries and Energy (1995) 63 FCR
567 at 585-587, applied.
Newcrest Mining (WA) Ltd v Commonwealth (1997) 190 CLR 513, dis-
tinguished.
(b) The power or the ability of the Commonwealth to act in the public interest or
for its own political purposes was not enhanced in a way that could be described as
a benefit to the Commonwealth by the s 10 declaration. (735)
(c) The s 10 declaration was not an exercise of legislative power pursuant to
s 51(xxxi) of the Constitution of the Commonwealth and was not a law with
respect to the acquisition of property. Accordingly, s 28 had no application. (747)

Of loss and assessment of damages
(34) It was not established that B Co suffered loss and damage by the making of
the invalid s 10 declaration. (847-851)

CASES CITED
